Ricketts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laura_Ricketts" title="Laura Ricketts">Laura Ricketts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pete_Ricketts" title="Pete Ricketts">Pete Ricketts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Todd_Ricketts" title="Todd Ricketts">Todd Ricketts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Ricketts" title="Joe Ricketts">Joe Ricketts</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Manager</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Joe_Maddon" title="Joe Maddon">Joe Maddon</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">General Manager</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Jed_Hoyer" title="Jed Hoyer">Jed Hoyer</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">President of Baseball Operations</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Theo_Epstein" title="Theo Epstein">Theo Epstein</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Chicago Cubs</b> are an American professional <a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a> team based in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago, Illinois</a>. The Cubs compete in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> (MLB) as a member club of the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> (NL) <a href="/wiki/National_League_Central" title="National League Central">Central</a> division. The team plays its home games at <a href="/wiki/Wrigley_Field" title="Wrigley Field">Wrigley Field</a>, located on the city\'s <a href="/wiki/Community_areas_in_Chicago#North_Side" title="Community areas in Chicago">North Side</a>. The Cubs are one of two major league teams in Chicago; the other, the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">Chicago White Sox</a>, is a member of the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> (AL) <a href="/wiki/American_League_Central" title="American League Central">Central</a> division. The Cubs, first known as the White Stockings, were a founding member of the NL in <a href="/wiki/1876_in_baseball" title="1876 in baseball">1876</a>, becoming the Chicago Cubs in <a href="/wiki/1903_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="1903 Chicago Cubs season">1903</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Cubs have appeared in a total of eleven World Series.  The <a href="/wiki/1906_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="1906 Chicago Cubs season">1906 Cubs</a> won 116 games, finishing 116\xe2\x80\x9336 and posting a modern-era record winning percentage of .763, before losing the <a href="/wiki/1906_World_Series" title="1906 World Series">World Series</a> to the <a href="/wiki/1906_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1906 Chicago White Sox season">Chicago White Sox</a> ("The Hitless Wonders") by four games to two. The Cubs won back-to-back World Series championships in <a href="/wiki/1907_World_Series" title="1907 World Series">1907</a> and <a href="/wiki/1908_World_Series" title="1908 World Series">1908</a>, becoming the first major league team to play in three consecutive World Series, and the first to win it twice.  Most recently, the Cubs won the <a href="/wiki/2016_National_League_Championship_Series" title="2016 National League Championship Series">2016 National League Championship Series</a> and <a href="/wiki/2016_World_Series" title="2016 World Series">2016 World Series</a>, which ended a 71-year <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_League_pennant_winners" title="List of National League pennant winners">National League pennant</a> drought and a 108-year World Series championship drought,<sup id="cite_ref-mlb_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mlb-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> both of which are record <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_franchise_postseason_droughts" title="List of Major League Baseball franchise postseason droughts">droughts in Major League Baseball</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Helyar_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Helyar-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nadel_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nadel-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The 108-year drought was also the longest such occurrence in all major North American sports. Since the start of divisional play in 1969, the Cubs have appeared in the postseason nine times through the 2017 season.<sup id="cite_ref-mlb_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mlb-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CubsWorldSeriesChamps2016_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CubsWorldSeriesChamps2016-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Cubs are known as "the North Siders", a reference to the location of Wrigley Field within the city of Chicago, and in contrast to the White Sox, whose home field (<a href="/wiki/Guaranteed_Rate_Field" title="Guaranteed Rate Field">Guaranteed Rate Field</a>) is located on the South Side.\n</p><p>The Cubs have multiple rivalries. There is a divisional <a href="/wiki/Cardinals%E2%80%93Cubs_rivalry" title="Cardinals\xe2\x80\x93Cubs rivalry">rivalry</a> with the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals" title="St. Louis Cardinals">St. Louis Cardinals</a>, a newer <a href="/wiki/Brewers%E2%80%93Cubs_rivalry" title="Brewers\xe2\x80\x93Cubs rivalry">rivalry</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Brewers" title="Milwaukee Brewers">Milwaukee Brewers</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Interleague_play" title="Interleague play">interleague</a> <a href="/wiki/Cubs%E2%80%93White_Sox_rivalry" title="Cubs\xe2\x80\x93White Sox rivalry">rivalry</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">Chicago White Sox</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Retired_numbers"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Retired numbers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a class="toctext">Media</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Radio"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Radio</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 Frick Award recipients</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Chicago_Cubs" title="History of the Chicago Cubs">History of the Chicago Cubs</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_club_history">Early club history</span></h3>\n<h4><span id="1876.E2.80.931902:_A_National_League"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1876\xe2\x80\x931902:_A_National_League">1876\xe2\x80\x931902: A National League</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1876_white_stockings.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/78/1876_white_stockings.jpg/160px-1876_white_stockings.jpg" width="160" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/78/1876_white_stockings.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="167" data-file-height="216" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1876_white_stockings.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1876 White Stockings won the N.L. championship</div></div></div>\n<p>The Cubs began play in 1870 as the <b>Chicago White Stockings</b>, joining the <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> (NL) in 1876 as a charter member. Owner <a href="/wiki/William_Hulbert" title="William Hulbert">William Hulbert</a> signed multiple star players, such as pitcher <a href="/wiki/Albert_Spalding" title="Albert Spalding">Albert Spalding</a> and infielders <a href="/wiki/Ross_Barnes" title="Ross Barnes">Ross Barnes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deacon_White" title="Deacon White">Deacon White</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cap_Anson" title="Cap Anson">Adrian "Cap" Anson</a>, to join the team prior to the N.L.\'s first season. The White Stockings played their home games at <a href="/wiki/West_Side_Grounds" class="mw-redirect" title="West Side Grounds">West Side Grounds</a> and quickly established themselves as one of the new league\'s top teams. Spalding won forty-seven games and Barnes led the league in hitting at .429 as Chicago won the first ever National League <a href="/wiki/Pennant_(sports)" title="Pennant (sports)">pennant</a>, which at the time was the game\'s top prize.\n</p><p>After back-to-back pennants in 1880 and 1881, Hulbert died, and Spalding, who had retired to start <a href="/wiki/Spalding_(sports_equipment)" class="mw-redirect" title="Spalding (sports equipment)">Spalding</a> sporting goods, assumed ownership of the club. The White Stockings, with Anson acting as player-manager, captured their third consecutive pennant in 1882, and Anson established himself as the game\'s first true superstar. In 1885 and \'86, after winning N.L. pennants, the White Stockings met the champions of the short-lived <a href="/wiki/American_Association_(19th_century)" title="American Association (19th century)">American Association</a> in that era\'s version of a World Series. Both seasons resulted in matchups with the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals" title="St. Louis Cardinals">St. Louis Brown Stockings</a>, with the clubs tying in 1885 and with St. Louis winning in 1886. This was the genesis of what would eventually become one of the greatest <a href="/wiki/Cardinals%E2%80%93Cubs_rivalry" title="Cardinals\xe2\x80\x93Cubs rivalry">rivalries</a> in sports. In all, the Anson-led Chicago Base Ball Club won six National League pennants between 1876 and 1886. As a result, Chicago\'s club nickname transitioned, and by <a href="/wiki/1890_in_baseball" title="1890 in baseball">1890</a> they had become known as the <b>Chicago Colts</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-1890colts_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1890colts-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> or sometimes "Anson\'s Colts", referring to Cap\'s influence within the club. Anson was the first player in history credited with collecting <a href="/wiki/3,000_hit_club" title="3,000 hit club">3,000 career hits</a>. After a disappointing record of 59\xe2\x80\x9373 and a ninth-place finish in 1897, Anson was released by the Cubs as both a player and manager.<sup id="cite_ref-google1_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Due to Anson\'s absence from the club after 22 years, local newspaper reporters started to refer to the Colts as the "Orphans".<sup id="cite_ref-google1_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/1900_in_baseball" title="1900 in baseball">1900</a> season, the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American Base-Ball League</a> formed as a rival professional league, and incidentally the club\'s old White Stockings nickname (eventually shortened to White Sox) would be adopted by <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">a new American League neighbor to the south</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1898orphans_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1898orphans-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1902.E2.80.931920:_A_Cubs_dynasty"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1902\xe2\x80\x931920:_A_Cubs_dynasty">1902\xe2\x80\x931920: A Cubs dynasty</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1906_Chicago_Cubs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1906_Chicago_Cubs.jpg/200px-1906_Chicago_Cubs.jpg" width="200" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1906_Chicago_Cubs.jpg/300px-1906_Chicago_Cubs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1906_Chicago_Cubs.jpg/400px-1906_Chicago_Cubs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1906_Chicago_Cubs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1906 Cubs won a record 116 of 154 games. They then won back-to-back World Series titles in 1907\xe2\x80\x9308</div></div></div><p> In 1902, Spalding, who by this time had revamped the roster to boast what would soon be one of the best teams of the early century, sold the club to <a href="/wiki/Jim_Hart_(baseball)" title="Jim Hart (baseball)">Jim Hart</a>. The franchise was nicknamed the Cubs by the <i>Chicago Daily News</i> in 1902, although not officially becoming the Chicago Cubs until the 1907 season.<sup id="cite_ref-1903cubs_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1903cubs-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> During this period, which has become known as baseball\'s <a href="/wiki/Dead-ball_era" title="Dead-ball era">dead-ball era</a>, Cub <a href="/wiki/Infielder" title="Infielder">infielders</a> <a href="/wiki/Joe_Tinker" title="Joe Tinker">Joe Tinker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Evers" title="Johnny Evers">Johnny Evers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Chance" title="Frank Chance">Frank Chance</a> were made famous as a double-play combination by <a href="/wiki/Franklin_P._Adams" title="Franklin P. Adams">Franklin P. Adams</a>\' poem <i><a href="/wiki/Baseball%27s_Sad_Lexicon" title="Baseball&#39;s Sad Lexicon">Baseball\'s Sad Lexicon</a></i>. The poem first appeared in the July 18, 1910 edition of the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Evening_Mail" title="New York Evening Mail">New York Evening Mail</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Mordecai_Brown" title="Mordecai Brown">Mordecai "Three-Finger" Brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Taylor_(20th_century_baseball_player)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack Taylor (20th century baseball player)">Jack Taylor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ed_Reulbach" title="Ed Reulbach">Ed Reulbach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Pfiester" title="Jack Pfiester">Jack Pfiester</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Orval_Overall" title="Orval Overall">Orval Overall</a> were several key pitchers for the Cubs during this time period. With Chance acting as player-manager from 1905 to 1912, the Cubs won four pennants and two World Series titles over a five-year span. Although they fell to the "Hitless Wonders" White Sox in the <a href="/wiki/1906_World_Series" title="1906 World Series">1906 World Series</a>, the Cubs recorded a record 116 victories and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_best_MLB_season_records" class="mw-redirect" title="List of best MLB season records">best winning percentage</a> (.763) in Major League history. With mostly the same roster, Chicago won back-to-back World Series championships in <a href="/wiki/1907_World_Series" title="1907 World Series">1907</a> and <a href="/wiki/1908_World_Series" title="1908 World Series">1908</a>, becoming the first Major League club to play three times in the Fall Classic and the first to win it twice. However, the Cubs would not win another World Series until <a href="/wiki/2016_World_Series" title="2016 World Series">2016</a>; this remains the longest championship drought in North American professional sports.\n</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1913_Chicago_Cubs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/1913_Chicago_Cubs.jpg/220px-1913_Chicago_Cubs.jpg" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/1913_Chicago_Cubs.jpg/330px-1913_Chicago_Cubs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/1913_Chicago_Cubs.jpg/440px-1913_Chicago_Cubs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3478" data-file-height="1838" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1913_Chicago_Cubs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div style="text-align: center;">1913 Chicago Cubs</div></div></div></div>\n<p>The next season, veteran catcher <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Kling" title="Johnny Kling">Johnny Kling</a> left the team to become a professional <a href="/wiki/Pocket_billiards" class="mw-redirect" title="Pocket billiards">pocket billiards</a> player. Some historians think Kling\'s absence was significant enough to prevent the Cubs from also winning a third straight title in 1909, as they finished 6 games out of first place.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> When Kling returned the next year, the Cubs won the <a href="/wiki/Pennant_(sports)" title="Pennant (sports)">pennant</a> again, but lost to the Philadelphia Athletics in the <a href="/wiki/1910_World_Series" title="1910 World Series">1910 World Series</a>.\n</p><p>In 1914, advertising executive <a href="/wiki/Albert_Lasker" title="Albert Lasker">Albert Lasker</a> obtained a large block of the club\'s shares and before the 1916 season assumed majority ownership of the franchise. Lasker brought in a wealthy partner, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Weeghman" title="Charles Weeghman">Charles Weeghman</a>, the proprietor of a popular chain of lunch counters who had previously owned the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Whales" title="Chicago Whales">Chicago Whales</a> of the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Federal_League" title="Federal League">Federal League</a>. As principal owners, the pair moved the club from the West Side Grounds to the much newer <a href="/wiki/Weeghman_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Weeghman Park">Weeghman Park</a>, which had been constructed for the Whales only two years earlier, where they remain to this day. The Cubs responded by winning a pennant in the war-shortened season of 1918, where they played a part in <a href="/wiki/Curse_of_the_Bambino" title="Curse of the Bambino">another team\'s curse</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a> defeated <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland_Alexander" title="Grover Cleveland Alexander">Grover Cleveland Alexander</a>\'s Cubs four games to two in the <a href="/wiki/1918_World_Series" title="1918 World Series">1918 World Series</a>, Boston\'s last Series championship until 2004.\n</p><p>Beginning in 1916, <a href="/wiki/William_Wrigley,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="William Wrigley, Jr.">Bill Wrigley</a> of chewing-gum fame acquired an increasing quantity of stock in the Cubs. By 1921 he was the majority owner, maintaining that status into the 1930s.\n</p><p>Meanwhile, the year 1919 saw the start of the tenure of <a href="/wiki/William_Veeck,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="William Veeck, Sr.">Bill Veeck, Sr.</a> as team president. Veeck would hold that post throughout the 1920s and into the 30s. The management team of Wrigley and Veeck came to be known as the "double-Bills."\n</p>\n<h3><span id="The_Wrigley_years_.281921.E2.80.931945.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Wrigley_years_(1921\xe2\x80\x931945)">The Wrigley years (1921\xe2\x80\x931945)</span></h3>\n<h4><span id="1929.E2.80.931938:_Every_three_years"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1929\xe2\x80\x931938:_Every_three_years">1929\xe2\x80\x931938: Every three years</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1920_cub_logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/1920_cub_logo.svg/120px-1920_cub_logo.svg.png" width="120" height="81" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/1920_cub_logo.svg/180px-1920_cub_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/1920_cub_logo.svg/240px-1920_cub_logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="133" data-file-height="90" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1920_cub_logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Club logo 1927\xe2\x80\x931936<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div><p>Near the end of the first decade of the double-Bills\' guidance, the Cubs won the NL pennant in 1929 and then achieved the unusual feat of winning a pennant every three years, following up the 1929 flag with league titles in 1932, 1935, and 1938. Unfortunately, their success did not extend to the <a href="/wiki/List_of_baseball_jargon_(F)" class="mw-redirect" title="List of baseball jargon (F)">Fall Classic</a>, as they fell to their <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">AL</a> rivals each time. The <a href="/wiki/1932_World_Series" title="1932 World Series">\'32 series</a> against the <a href="/wiki/1932_New_York_Yankees_season" title="1932 New York Yankees season">Yankees</a> featured Babe Ruth\'s "<a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth%27s_called_shot" title="Babe Ruth&#39;s called shot">called shot</a>" at Wrigley Field in game three. There were some historic moments for the Cubs as well; In 1930, <a href="/wiki/Hack_Wilson" title="Hack Wilson">Hack Wilson</a>, one of the top home run hitters in the game, had one of the most impressive seasons in MLB history, hitting 56 home runs and establishing the current runs-batted-in record of 191. That 1930 club, which boasted six eventual hall of fame members (Wilson, <a href="/wiki/Gabby_Hartnett" title="Gabby Hartnett">Gabby Hartnett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Hornsby" title="Rogers Hornsby">Rogers Hornsby</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Kelly_(baseball)" title="George Kelly (baseball)">George "High Pockets" Kelly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kiki_Cuyler" title="Kiki Cuyler">Kiki Cuyler</a> and manager <a href="/wiki/Joe_McCarthy_(manager)" title="Joe McCarthy (manager)">Joe McCarthy</a>) established the current team batting average record of .309. In 1935 the Cubs claimed the pennant in thrilling fashion, winning a record 21 games in a row in September. The <a href="/wiki/1938_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="1938 Chicago Cubs season">\'38 club</a> saw <a href="/wiki/Dizzy_Dean" title="Dizzy Dean">Dizzy Dean</a> lead the team\'s pitching staff and provided a historic moment when they won a crucial late-season game at Wrigley Field over the <a href="/wiki/1938_Pittsburgh_Pirates_season" title="1938 Pittsburgh Pirates season">Pittsburgh Pirates</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Walk-off_home_run" title="Walk-off home run">walk-off home run</a> by Gabby Hartnett, which became known in baseball <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">lore</a> as "<a href="/wiki/The_Homer_in_the_Gloamin%27" class="mw-redirect" title="The Homer in the Gloamin&#39;">The Homer in the Gloamin\'</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the "double-Bills" (Wrigley and Veeck) died in 1932 and 1933 respectively, <a href="/wiki/P.K._Wrigley" class="mw-redirect" title="P.K. Wrigley">P.K. Wrigley</a>, son of Bill Wrigley, took over as majority owner. He was unable to extend his father\'s baseball success beyond 1938, and the Cubs slipped into years of mediocrity, although the Wrigley family would retain control of the team until 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1945.2FThe_Curse_of_the_Billy_Goat"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1945/The_Curse_of_the_Billy_Goat">1945/The Curse of the Billy Goat</span></h4>\n<p>The Cubs enjoyed one more pennant at the close of World War II, finishing 98\xe2\x80\x9356. Due to the wartime travel restrictions, the first three games of the <a href="/wiki/1945_World_Series" title="1945 World Series">1945 World Series</a> were played in <a href="/wiki/Tiger_Stadium_(Detroit)" title="Tiger Stadium (Detroit)">Detroit</a>, where the Cubs won two games, including a one-hitter by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Passeau" title="Claude Passeau">Claude Passeau</a>, and the final four were played at Wrigley. The Cubs lost the series, and did not return until the <a href="/wiki/2016_World_Series" title="2016 World Series">2016 World Series</a>. After losing the 1945 World Series to the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit Tigers</a>, the Cubs finished with a respectable 82-71 record in the following year, but this was only good enough for third place.\n</p><p>In the following two decades, the Cubs played mostly forgettable baseball, finishing among the worst teams in the National League on an almost annual basis. From 1947 to 1966, they only notched one winning season. Longtime infielder-manager <a href="/wiki/Phil_Cavarretta" title="Phil Cavarretta">Phil Cavarretta</a>, who had been a key player during the 1945 season, was fired during spring training in 1954 after admitting the team was unlikely to finish above fifth place. Although shortstop <a href="/wiki/Ernie_Banks" title="Ernie Banks">Ernie Banks</a> would become one of the star players in the league during the next decade, finding help for him proved a difficult task, as quality players such as <a href="/wiki/Hank_Sauer" title="Hank Sauer">Hank Sauer</a> were few and far between. This, combined with poor ownership decisions such as the <a href="/wiki/College_of_Coaches" title="College of Coaches">College of Coaches</a>, and the ill-fated trade of future <a href="/wiki/National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum" title="National Baseball Hall of Fame and Museum">Hall of Fame</a> member <a href="/wiki/Lou_Brock" title="Lou Brock">Lou Brock</a> to the Cardinals for pitcher <a href="/wiki/Ernie_Broglio" title="Ernie Broglio">Ernie Broglio</a> (who won only seven games over the next three seasons), hampered on-field performance.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1969:_Fall_of_.2769"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1969:_Fall_of_\'69">1969: Fall of \'69</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1969_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="1969 Chicago Cubs season">1969 Chicago Cubs season</a></div>\n<p>The late-1960s brought hope of a renaissance, with third baseman <a href="/wiki/Ron_Santo" title="Ron Santo">Ron Santo</a>, pitcher <a href="/wiki/Ferguson_Jenkins" title="Ferguson Jenkins">Ferguson Jenkins</a>, and outfielder <a href="/wiki/Billy_Williams_(left_fielder)" class="mw-redirect" title="Billy Williams (left fielder)">Billy Williams</a> joining Banks. After losing a dismal 103 games in 1966, the Cubs brought home consecutive winning records in <a href="/wiki/1967_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1967 Major League Baseball season">\'67</a> and <a href="/wiki/1968_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1968 Major League Baseball season">\'68</a>, marking the first time a Cub team had accomplished that feat in over two decades.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/1969_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1969 Major League Baseball season">1969</a> the Cubs, managed by <a href="/wiki/Leo_Durocher" title="Leo Durocher">Leo Durocher</a>, built a substantial lead in the newly created <a href="/wiki/National_League_East" title="National League East">National League Eastern</a> Division by mid-August. <a href="/wiki/Ken_Holtzman" title="Ken Holtzman">Ken Holtzman</a> pitched a no-hitter on August 19, and the division lead grew to 8 &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> games over the St. Louis Cardinals and by 9 &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> games over the <a href="/wiki/1969_New_York_Mets_season" title="1969 New York Mets season">New York Mets</a>. After the game of September 2, the Cubs record was 84-52 with the Mets in second place at 77-55. But then a losing streak began just as a Mets winning streak was beginning. The Cubs lost the final game of a series at Cincinnati, then came home to play the resurgent Pittsburgh Pirates (who would finish in third place). After losing the first two games by scores of 9-2 and 13-4, the Cubs led going into the ninth inning. A win would be a positive springboard since the Cubs were to play a crucial series with the Mets the next day. But Willie Stargell drilled a two-out, two-strike pitch from the Cubs\' ace reliever, Phil Regan, onto Sheffield Avenue to tie the score in the top of the ninth. The Cubs would lose 7-5 in extra innings.[6]\nBurdened by a four-game losing streak, the Cubs traveled to Shea Stadium for a short two-game set. The Mets won both games, and the Cubs left New York with a record of 84-58 just 1\xe2\x81\x842 game in front. More of the same followed in Philadelphia, as a 99 loss Phillies team nonetheless defeated the Cubs twice, to extend Chicago\'s losing streak to eight games. In a key play in the second game, on September 11, Cubs starter Dick Selma threw a surprise pickoff attempt to third baseman Ron Santo, who was nowhere near the bag or the ball. Selma\'s throwing error opened the gates to a Phillies rally.\nAfter that second Philly loss, the Cubs were 84-60 and the Mets had pulled ahead at 85-57. The Mets would not look back. The Cubs\' eight-game losing streak finally ended the next day in St. Louis, but the Mets were in the midst of a ten-game winning streak, and the Cubs, wilting from team fatigue, generally deteriorated in all phases of the game.[1] The Mets (who had lost a record 120 games 7 years earlier), would go on to win the World Series. The Cubs, despite a respectable 92-70 record, would be remembered for having lost a remarkable 17\xc2\xbd games in the standings to the Mets in the last quarter of the season.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1977.E2.80.931979:_June_Swoon"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1977\xe2\x80\x931979:_June_Swoon">1977\xe2\x80\x931979: June Swoon</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1977_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="1977 Chicago Cubs season">1977 Chicago Cubs season</a></div>\n<p>Following the 1969 season, the club posted winning records for the next few seasons, but no playoff action. After the core players of those teams started to move on, the 70s got worse for the team, and they became known as "the Loveable Losers." In <a href="/wiki/1977_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1977 Major League Baseball season">1977</a>, the team found some life, but ultimately experienced one of its <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.baseball-reference.com/teams/CHC/1977.shtml">biggest collapses.</a> The Cubs hit a high-water mark on June 28 at 47\xe2\x80\x9322, boasting an &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap">8<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> game NL East lead, as they were led by <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Murcer" title="Bobby Murcer">Bobby Murcer</a> (27 HR/89 RBI), and <a href="/wiki/Rick_Reuschel" title="Rick Reuschel">Rick Reuschel</a> (20\xe2\x80\x9310). However, the <a href="/wiki/1977_Philadelphia_Phillies_season" title="1977 Philadelphia Phillies season">Philadelphia Phillies</a> cut the lead to two by the All-star break, as the Cubs sat 19 games over .500, but they swooned late in the season, going 20\xe2\x80\x9340 after July 31. The Cubs finished in fourth place at 81\xe2\x80\x9381, while Philadelphia surged, finishing with 101 wins. The following two seasons also saw the Cubs get off to a fast start, as the team rallied to over 10 games above .500 well into both seasons, only to again wear down and play poorly later on, and ultimately settling back to mediocrity. This trait became known as the "June Swoon". Again, the Cubs\' unusually high number of day games is often pointed to as one reason for the team\'s inconsistent late season play.\n</p><p>Wrigley died in 1977. The Wrigley family sold the team to the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a> in 1981, ending a 65-year family relationship with the Cubs.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Tribune_Company_years_.281981.E2.80.932008.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tribune_Company_years_(1981\xe2\x80\x932008)">Tribune Company years (1981\xe2\x80\x932008)</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1984:_Heartbreak">1984: Heartbreak</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1984_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="1984 Chicago Cubs season">1984 Chicago Cubs season</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pic_of_ryne_sandberg_from_the_early_90%27s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pic_of_ryne_sandberg_from_the_early_90%27s.jpg/150px-Pic_of_ryne_sandberg_from_the_early_90%27s.jpg" width="150" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pic_of_ryne_sandberg_from_the_early_90%27s.jpg/225px-Pic_of_ryne_sandberg_from_the_early_90%27s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fb/Pic_of_ryne_sandberg_from_the_early_90%27s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="277" data-file-height="408" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pic_of_ryne_sandberg_from_the_early_90%27s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ryne Sandberg set numerous league and club records in his career and was elected to the Hall of Fame in 2005.</div></div></div><p>After over a dozen more subpar seasons, in 1981 the Cubs hired GM <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Green_(baseball)" title="Dallas Green (baseball)">Dallas Green</a> from Philadelphia to turn around the franchise. Green had managed the 1980 Phillies to the World Series title. One of his early GM moves brought in a young Phillies minor-league 3rd baseman named Ryne Sandberg, along with Larry Bowa for Iv\xc3\xa1n DeJes\xc3\xbas. The 1983 Cubs had finished 71\xe2\x80\x9391 under Lee Elia, who was fired before the season ended by Green. Green continued the culture of change and overhauled the Cubs roster, front-office and coaching staff prior to 1984. <a href="/wiki/Jim_Frey" title="Jim Frey">Jim Frey</a> was hired to manage the 1984 Cubs, with <a href="/wiki/Don_Zimmer" title="Don Zimmer">Don Zimmer</a> coaching 3rd base and <a href="/wiki/Billy_Connors" title="Billy Connors">Billy Connors</a> serving as pitching coach.\n</p><p>Green shored<sup id="cite_ref-1984Cubs_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1984Cubs-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> up the 1984 roster with a series of transactions. In December, 1983 <a href="/wiki/Scott_Sanderson_(baseball)" title="Scott Sanderson (baseball)">Scott Sanderson</a> was acquired from Montreal in a three-team deal with San Diego for <a href="/wiki/Carmelo_Mart%C3%ADnez" title="Carmelo Mart\xc3\xadnez">Carmelo Mart\xc3\xadnez</a>. Pinch hitter <a href="/wiki/Richie_Hebner" title="Richie Hebner">Richie Hebner</a> (.333 BA in 1984) was signed as a free-agent. In spring training, moves continued: LF <a href="/wiki/Gary_Matthews" title="Gary Matthews">Gary Matthews</a> and CF <a href="/wiki/Bobby_Dernier" class="mw-redirect" title="Bobby Dernier">Bobby Dernier</a> came from Philadelphia on March 26, for <a href="/wiki/Bill_Campbell_(baseball)" title="Bill Campbell (baseball)">Bill Campbell</a> and a minor leaguer. Reliever Tim Stoddard (10\xe2\x80\x936 3.82, 7 saves) was acquired the same day for a minor leaguer; veteran pitcher <a href="/wiki/Ferguson_Jenkins" title="Ferguson Jenkins">Ferguson Jenkins</a> was released.\n</p><p>The team\'s commitment to contend was complete when Green made a midseason deal on June 15 to shore up the starting rotation due to injuries to <a href="/wiki/Rick_Reuschel" title="Rick Reuschel">Rick Reuschel</a> (5\xe2\x80\x935) and Sanderson. The deal brought 1979 NL Rookie of the Year pitcher <a href="/wiki/Rick_Sutcliffe" title="Rick Sutcliffe">Rick Sutcliffe</a> from the Cleveland Indians. <a href="/wiki/Joe_Carter" title="Joe Carter">Joe Carter</a> (who was with the Triple-A <a href="/wiki/Iowa_Cubs" title="Iowa Cubs">Iowa Cubs</a> at the time) and right fielder <a href="/wiki/Mel_Hall" title="Mel Hall">Mel Hall</a> were sent to Cleveland for Sutcliffe and back-up catcher Ron Hassey (.333 with Cubs in 1984). Sutcliffe (5\xe2\x80\x935 with the Indians) immediately joined Sanderson (8\xe2\x80\x935 3.14), Eckersley (10\xe2\x80\x938 3.03), <a href="/wiki/Steve_Trout" title="Steve Trout">Steve Trout</a> (13\xe2\x80\x937 3.41) and <a href="/wiki/Dick_Ruthven" title="Dick Ruthven">Dick Ruthven</a> (6\xe2\x80\x9310 5.04) in the starting rotation. Sutcliffe proceeded to go 16\xe2\x80\x931 for Cubs and capture the <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young_Award" title="Cy Young Award">Cy Young Award</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1984Cubs_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1984Cubs-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Cubs 1984 starting lineup was very strong.<sup id="cite_ref-1984Cubs_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1984Cubs-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> It consisted of LF Matthews (.291 14\xe2\x80\x9382 101 runs 17 SB), C <a href="/wiki/Jody_Davis_(baseball)" title="Jody Davis (baseball)">Jody Davis</a> (.256 19\xe2\x80\x9394), RF <a href="/wiki/Keith_Moreland" title="Keith Moreland">Keith Moreland</a> (.279 16\xe2\x80\x9380), SS <a href="/wiki/Larry_Bowa" title="Larry Bowa">Larry Bowa</a> (.223 10 SB), 1B <a href="/wiki/Leon_%22Bull%22_Durham" class="mw-redirect" title="Leon &quot;Bull&quot; Durham">Leon "Bull" Durham</a> (.279 23\xe2\x80\x9396 16SB), CF Dernier (.278 45 SB), 3B <a href="/wiki/Ron_Cey" title="Ron Cey">Ron Cey</a> (.240 25\xe2\x80\x9397), Closer <a href="/wiki/Lee_Smith_(baseball)" title="Lee Smith (baseball)">Lee Smith</a>(9\xe2\x80\x937 3.65 33 saves) and 1984 NL MVP <a href="/wiki/Ryne_Sandberg" title="Ryne Sandberg">Ryne Sandberg</a> (.314 19\xe2\x80\x9384 114 runs, 19 triples,32 SB).<sup id="cite_ref-1984Cubs_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1984Cubs-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Reserve players Hebner, <a href="/wiki/Thad_Bosley" title="Thad Bosley">Thad Bosley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cotto" title="Henry Cotto">Henry Cotto</a>, Hassey and <a href="/wiki/Dave_Owen_(baseball)" title="Dave Owen (baseball)">Dave Owen</a> produced exciting moments. The bullpen depth of <a href="/wiki/Rich_Bordi" title="Rich Bordi">Rich Bordi</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Frazier_(pitcher)" title="George Frazier (pitcher)">George Frazier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warren_Brusstar" title="Warren Brusstar">Warren Brusstar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dickie_Noles" title="Dickie Noles">Dickie Noles</a> did their job in getting the game to Smith or Stoddard.\n</p><p>At the top of the order, Dernier and Sandberg were exciting, aptly coined "the Daily Double" by <a href="/wiki/Harry_Caray" title="Harry Caray">Harry Caray</a>. With strong defense \xe2\x80\x93 Dernier CF and Sandberg 2B, won the NL <a href="/wiki/Rawlings_Gold_Glove_Award" title="Rawlings Gold Glove Award">Gold Glove</a>- solid pitching and clutch hitting, the Cubs were a well balanced team. Following the "Daily Double", Matthews, Durham, Cey, Moreland and Davis gave the Cubs an order with no gaps to pitch around. Sutcliffe anchored a strong top-to-bottom rotation, and Smith was one of the top closers in the game.\n</p><p>The shift in the Cubs\' fortunes was characterized June 23 on the "NBC Saturday Game of the Week" contest against the St. Louis Cardinals; it has since been dubbed simply "<a href="/wiki/The_Sandberg_Game" class="mw-redirect" title="The Sandberg Game">The Sandberg Game</a>." With the nation watching and Wrigley Field packed, Sandberg emerged as a superstar with not one, but two game-tying home runs against Cardinals closer <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Sutter" title="Bruce Sutter">Bruce Sutter</a>. With his shots in the 9th and 10th innings Wrigley Field erupted and Sandberg set the stage for a comeback win that cemented the Cubs as the team to beat in the East. No one would catch them.\n</p><p>In early August the Cubs swept the Mets in a 4-game home series that further distanced them from the pack. An infamous Keith Moreland-<a href="/wiki/Ed_Lynch_(baseball)" title="Ed Lynch (baseball)">Ed Lynch</a> fight erupted after Lynch hit Moreland with a pitch, perhaps forgetting Moreland was once a linebacker at the University of Texas. It was the second game of a double header and the Cubs had won the first game in part due to a three run home run by Moreland. After the bench-clearing fight the Cubs won the second game, and the sweep put the Cubs at 68\xe2\x80\x9345.\n</p><p>In 1984, each league had two divisions, East and West. The divisional winners met in a best-of-5 series to advance to the World Series, in a "2\xe2\x80\x933" format, first two games were played at the home of the team who did not have home field advantage. Then the last three games were played at the home of the team, with home field advantage. Thus the first two games were played at Wrigley Field and the next three at the home of their opponents, San Diego. A common and unfounded myth is that since Wrigley Field did not have lights at that time the National League decided to give the home field advantage to the winner of the NL West. In fact, home field advantage had rotated between the winners of the East and West since 1969 when the league expanded. In even numbered years, the NL West had home field advantage. In odd numbered years, the NL East had home field advantage. Since the NL East winners had had home field advantage in 1983, the NL West winners were entitled to it.\n</p><p>The confusion may stem from the fact that Major League Baseball did decide that, should the Cubs make it to the World Series, the American League winner would have home field advantage unless the Cubs hosted home games at an alternate site since the Cubs home field of Wrigley Field did not yet have lights. Rumor was the Cubs could hold home games across town at Comiskey Park, home of the American League\'s Chicago White Sox. Rather than hold any games in the cross town rival Sox Park, the Cubs made arrangements with the August A. Busch, owner of the St. Louis Cardinals, to use Busch Stadium in St. Louis as the Cubs "home field" for the World Series. This was approved by Major League Baseball and would have enabled the Cubs to host games 1 and 2, along with games 6 and 7 if necessary. At the time home field advantage was rotated between each league. Odd numbered years the AL had home field advantage. Even numbered years the NL had home field advantage. In the 1982 World Series the St. Louis Cardinals of the NL had home field advantage. In the 1983 World Series the Baltimore Orioles of the AL had home field advantage.\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/1984_National_League_Championship_Series" title="1984 National League Championship Series">NLCS</a>, the Cubs easily won the first two games at Wrigley Field against the <a href="/wiki/1984_San_Diego_Padres_season" title="1984 San Diego Padres season">San Diego Padres</a>. The Padres were the winners of the Western Division with <a href="/wiki/Steve_Garvey" title="Steve Garvey">Steve Garvey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Gwynn" title="Tony Gwynn">Tony Gwynn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Show" title="Eric Show">Eric Show</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goose_Gossage" title="Goose Gossage">Goose Gossage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Wiggins" title="Alan Wiggins">Alan Wiggins</a>. With wins of 13\xe2\x80\x930 and 4\xe2\x80\x932, the Cubs needed to win only one game of the next three in San Diego to make it to the World Series. After being beaten in Game 3 7\xe2\x80\x931, the Cubs lost Game 4 when Smith, with the game tied 5\xe2\x80\x935, allowed a game-winning home run to Garvey in the bottom of the ninth inning. In Game 5 the Cubs took a 3\xe2\x80\x930 lead into the 6th inning, and a 3\xe2\x80\x932 lead into the seventh with Sutcliffe (who won the <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young_Award" title="Cy Young Award">Cy Young Award</a> that year) still on the mound. Then, Leon Durham had a sharp grounder go under his glove. This critical error helped the Padres win the game 6\xe2\x80\x933, with a 4-run 7th inning and keep Chicago out of the <a href="/wiki/1984_World_Series" title="1984 World Series">1984 World Series</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit Tigers</a>. The loss ended a spectacular season for the Cubs, one that brought alive a slumbering franchise and made the Cubs relevant for a whole new generation of Cubs fans.\n</p><p>The Padres would be defeated in 5 games by Sparky Anderson\'s Tigers in the World Series.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shawon_Dunston.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Shawon_Dunston.jpg/175px-Shawon_Dunston.jpg" width="175" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Shawon_Dunston.jpg/263px-Shawon_Dunston.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Shawon_Dunston.jpg/350px-Shawon_Dunston.jpg 2x" data-file-width="702" data-file-height="1012" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shawon_Dunston.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shawon Dunston was the Cubs shortstop for 10 years.</div></div></div><p>The 1985 season brought high hopes. The club started out well, going 35\xe2\x80\x9319 through mid-June, but injuries to Sutcliffe and others in the pitching staff contributed to a 13-game losing streak that pushed the Cubs out of contention.\n</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1989:_NL_East_division_championship">1989: NL East division championship</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1989_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="1989 Chicago Cubs season">1989 Chicago Cubs season</a></div>\n<p>In 1989, the first full season with night baseball at Wrigley Field, <a href="/wiki/Don_Zimmer" title="Don Zimmer">Don Zimmer</a>\'s Cubs were led by a core group of veterans in <a href="/wiki/Ryne_Sandberg" title="Ryne Sandberg">Ryne Sandberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rick_Sutcliffe" title="Rick Sutcliffe">Rick Sutcliffe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andre_Dawson" title="Andre Dawson">Andre Dawson</a>, who were boosted by a crop of youngsters such as <a href="/wiki/Mark_Grace" title="Mark Grace">Mark Grace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shawon_Dunston" title="Shawon Dunston">Shawon Dunston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greg_Maddux" title="Greg Maddux">Greg Maddux</a>, Rookie of the Year <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Walton" title="Jerome Walton">Jerome Walton</a>, and Rookie of the Year Runner-Up Dwight Smith. The Cubs won the NL East once again that season winning 93 games. This time the Cubs met the <a href="/wiki/1989_San_Francisco_Giants_season" title="1989 San Francisco Giants season">San Francisco Giants</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1989_National_League_Championship_Series" title="1989 National League Championship Series">NLCS</a>. After splitting the first two games at home, the Cubs headed to the Bay Area, where despite holding a lead at some point in each of the next three games, bullpen meltdowns and managerial blunders ultimately led to three straight losses. The Cubs couldn\'t overcome the efforts of Will Clark, whose home run off Maddux, just after a managerial visit to the mound, led Maddux to think Clark knew what pitch was coming. Afterward, Maddux would speak into his glove during any mound conversation, beginning what is a norm today. Mark Grace was 11\xe2\x80\x9317 in the series with 8 RBI. Eventually, the Giants lost to the "<a href="/wiki/Bash_Brothers" title="Bash Brothers">Bash Brothers</a>" and the <a href="/wiki/1989_Oakland_Athletics_season" title="1989 Oakland Athletics season">Oakland A\'s</a> in the famous "<i><a href="/wiki/1989_World_Series" title="1989 World Series">Earthquake Series</a></i>."\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1998:_Wild_card_race_and_home_run_chase">1998: Wild card race and home run chase</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Howmanyout.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Howmanyout.JPG/170px-Howmanyout.JPG" width="170" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Howmanyout.JPG/255px-Howmanyout.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/Howmanyout.JPG 2x" data-file-width="330" data-file-height="304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Howmanyout.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sammy Sosa was the captain of the Chicago Cubs during his tenure with the team.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/1998_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="1998 Chicago Cubs season">1998 Chicago Cubs season</a> and <a href="/wiki/1998_Major_League_Baseball_home_run_record_chase" title="1998 Major League Baseball home run record chase">1998 Major League Baseball home run record chase</a></div>\n<p>The 1998 season would begin on a somber note with the death of legendary broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Harry_Caray" title="Harry Caray">Harry Caray</a>. After the retirement of Sandberg and the trade of Dunston, the Cubs had holes to fill, and the signing of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Rodr%C3%ADguez_(outfielder)" title="Henry Rodr\xc3\xadguez (outfielder)">Henry Rodr\xc3\xadguez</a> to bat cleanup provided protection for <a href="/wiki/Sammy_Sosa" title="Sammy Sosa">Sammy Sosa</a> in the lineup, as Rodriguez slugged 31 round-trippers in his first season in Chicago. <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Tapani" title="Kevin Tapani">Kevin Tapani</a> led the club with a career high 19 wins while <a href="/wiki/Rod_Beck" title="Rod Beck">Rod Beck</a> anchored a strong bullpen and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Grace" title="Mark Grace">Mark Grace</a> turned in one of his best seasons. The Cubs were swamped by media attention in 1998, and the team\'s two biggest headliners were Sosa and rookie flamethrower <a href="/wiki/Kerry_Wood" title="Kerry Wood">Kerry Wood</a>. Wood\'s signature performance was one-hitting the <a href="/wiki/1998_Houston_Astros_season" title="1998 Houston Astros season">Houston Astros</a>, a game in which he tied the major league record of 20 strikeouts in nine innings. His torrid strikeout numbers earned Wood the nickname <i>"Kid K,"</i> and ultimately earned him the 1998 <a href="/wiki/NL_Rookie_of_the_Year" class="mw-redirect" title="NL Rookie of the Year">NL Rookie of the Year</a> award. Sosa caught fire in June, hitting a major league record 20 home runs in the month, and his home run race with Cardinals slugger <a href="/wiki/Mark_McGwire" title="Mark McGwire">Mark McGwire</a> transformed the pair into international superstars in a matter of weeks. McGwire finished the season with a new major league record of 70 home runs, but Sosa\'s .308 average and 66 homers earned him the <a href="/wiki/National_League_MVP_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="National League MVP Award">National League MVP Award</a>. After a down-to-the-wire <a href="/wiki/Wild_card_(sports)" title="Wild card (sports)">Wild Card</a> chase with the <a href="/wiki/1998_San_Francisco_Giants_season" title="1998 San Francisco Giants season">San Francisco Giants</a>, Chicago and San Francisco ended the regular season tied, and thus squared off in a one-game playoff at Wrigley Field. Third baseman <a href="/wiki/Gary_Gaetti" title="Gary Gaetti">Gary Gaetti</a> hit the eventual game winning homer in the playoff game. The win propelled the Cubs into the postseason for the first time since 1989 with a 90\xe2\x80\x9373 regular season record. Unfortunately, the bats went cold in October, as manager <a href="/wiki/Jim_Riggleman" title="Jim Riggleman">Jim Riggleman</a>\'s club batted .183 and scored only four runs en route to being swept by <a href="/wiki/1998_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1998 Atlanta Braves season">Atlanta</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1998_National_League_Division_Series" title="1998 National League Division Series">National League Division Series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The home run chase between Sosa, McGwire and <a href="/wiki/Ken_Griffey,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Ken Griffey, Jr.">Ken Griffey, Jr.</a> helped professional baseball to bring in a new crop of fans as well as bringing back some fans who had been disillusioned by the <a href="/wiki/1994%E2%80%9395_Major_League_Baseball_strike" title="1994\xe2\x80\x9395 Major League Baseball strike">1994 strike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The Cubs retained many players who experienced career years in 1998, but, after a fast start in 1999, they collapsed again (starting with being swept at the hands of the cross-town <a href="/wiki/1998_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1998 Chicago White Sox season">White Sox</a> in mid-June) and finished in the bottom of the division for the next two seasons.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2001:_Playoff_push">2001: Playoff push</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2001_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2001 Chicago Cubs season">2001 Chicago Cubs season</a></div>\n<p>Despite losing fan favorite Grace to free agency and the lack of production from newcomer <a href="/wiki/Todd_Hundley" title="Todd Hundley">Todd Hundley</a>, skipper <a href="/wiki/Don_Baylor" title="Don Baylor">Don Baylor</a>\'s Cubs put together a good season in 2001. The <a href="/wiki/2001_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="2001 Major League Baseball season">season</a> started with Mack Newton being brought in to preach "positive thinking." One of the biggest stories of the season transpired as the club made a midseason deal for <a href="/wiki/Fred_McGriff" title="Fred McGriff">Fred McGriff</a>, which was drawn out for nearly a month as McGriff debated waiving his <a href="/wiki/No-trade_clause" class="mw-redirect" title="No-trade clause">no-trade clause</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The Cubs led the wild card race by 2.5 games in early September, but crumbled when <a href="/wiki/Preston_Wilson" title="Preston Wilson">Preston Wilson</a> hit a three run walk off homer off of closer <a href="/wiki/Tom_Gordon" title="Tom Gordon">Tom "Flash" Gordon</a>, which halted the team\'s momentum. The team was unable to make another serious charge, and finished at 88\xe2\x80\x9374, five games behind both <a href="/wiki/2001_Houston_Astros_season" title="2001 Houston Astros season">Houston</a> and <a href="/wiki/2001_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="2001 St. Louis Cardinals season">St. Louis</a>, who tied for first. Sosa had perhaps his finest season and <a href="/wiki/Jon_Lieber" title="Jon Lieber">Jon Lieber</a> led the staff with a 20-win season.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2003:_Five_more_outs">2003: Five more outs</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/2003_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2003 Chicago Cubs season">2003 Chicago Cubs season</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steve_Bartman_incident" title="Steve Bartman incident">Steve Bartman incident</a></div>\n<p>The Cubs had high expectations in 2002, but the squad played poorly. On July 5, 2002, the Cubs promoted assistant general manager and player personnel director <a href="/wiki/Jim_Hendry" title="Jim Hendry">Jim Hendry</a> to the General Manager position. The club responded by hiring <a href="/wiki/Dusty_Baker" title="Dusty Baker">Dusty Baker</a> and by making some major moves in 2003. Most notably, they traded with the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Pirates" title="Pittsburgh Pirates">Pittsburgh Pirates</a> for outfielder <a href="/wiki/Kenny_Lofton" title="Kenny Lofton">Kenny Lofton</a> and third baseman <a href="/wiki/Aramis_Ram%C3%ADrez" title="Aramis Ram\xc3\xadrez">Aramis Ram\xc3\xadrez</a>, and rode dominant pitching, led by Kerry Wood and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Prior" title="Mark Prior">Mark Prior</a>, as the Cubs led the division down the stretch.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:146px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mark_Prior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mark_Prior.jpg/144px-Mark_Prior.jpg" width="144" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mark_Prior.jpg/216px-Mark_Prior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mark_Prior.jpg/288px-Mark_Prior.jpg 2x" data-file-width="417" data-file-height="799" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mark_Prior.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mark Prior, along with Kerry Wood, led the Cubs\' rotation in 2003.</div></div></div>\n<p>Chicago halted St. Louis\' run to the playoffs by taking four of five games from the Cardinals at Wrigley Field in early September, after which they won their first division title in 14 years. They then went on to defeat the <a href="/wiki/2003_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="2003 Atlanta Braves season">Atlanta Braves</a> in a dramatic five-game <a href="/wiki/2003_National_League_Division_Series" title="2003 National League Division Series">Division Series</a>, the franchise\'s first postseason series win since beating the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit Tigers</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1908_World_Series" title="1908 World Series">1908 World Series</a>.\n</p><p>After losing an extra-inning game in Game 1, the Cubs rallied and took a three-games-to-one lead over the Wild Card <a href="/wiki/2003_Florida_Marlins_season" title="2003 Florida Marlins season">Florida Marlins</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2003_National_League_Championship_Series" title="2003 National League Championship Series">National League Championship Series</a>. Florida shut the Cubs out in Game 5, but the Cubs returned home to Wrigley Field with young pitcher <a href="/wiki/Mark_Prior" title="Mark Prior">Mark Prior</a> to lead the Cubs in Game 6 as they took a 3\xe2\x80\x930 lead into the 8th inning. It was at this point when a now-infamous <a href="/wiki/Steve_Bartman_incident" title="Steve Bartman incident">incident</a> took place. Several spectators attempted to catch a foul ball off the bat of <a href="/wiki/Luis_Castillo_(baseball)" class="mw-redirect" title="Luis Castillo (baseball)">Luis Castillo</a>. A Chicago Cubs fan by the name of <a href="/wiki/Steve_Bartman_incident" title="Steve Bartman incident">Steve Bartman</a>, of Northbrook, Illinois, reached for the ball and deflected it away from the glove of <a href="/wiki/Mois%C3%A9s_Alou" title="Mois\xc3\xa9s Alou">Mois\xc3\xa9s Alou</a> for the second out of the eighth inning. Alou reacted angrily toward the stands and after the game stated that he would have caught the ball.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartman_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartman-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Alou at one point recanted, saying he would not have been able to make the play, but later said this was just an attempt to make Bartman feel better and believing the whole incident should be forgotten.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartman_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartman-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Interference was not called on the play, as the ball was ruled to be on the spectator side of the wall. Castillo was eventually walked by Prior. Two batters later, and to the chagrin of the packed stadium, Cubs shortstop <a href="/wiki/Alex_Gonzalez_(shortstop,_born_1973)" title="Alex Gonzalez (shortstop, born 1973)">Alex Gonzalez</a> misplayed an inning-ending double play, loading the bases. The error would lead to eight Florida runs and a Marlin victory. Despite sending <a href="/wiki/Kerry_Wood" title="Kerry Wood">Kerry Wood</a> to the mound and holding a lead twice, the Cubs ultimately dropped Game 7, and failed to reach the <a href="/wiki/2003_World_Series" title="2003 World Series">World Series</a>.\n</p><p>The "Steve Bartman incident" was seen as the "first domino" in the turning point of the era, and the Cubs did not win a playoff game for the next eleven seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="2004.E2.80.932006"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2004\xe2\x80\x932006">2004\xe2\x80\x932006</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/2004_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2004 Chicago Cubs season">2004 Chicago Cubs season</a>, <a href="/wiki/2005_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2005 Chicago Cubs season">2005 Chicago Cubs season</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2006_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2006 Chicago Cubs season">2006 Chicago Cubs season</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/2004_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="2004 Major League Baseball season">2004</a>, the Cubs were a consensus pick by most media outlets to win the World Series. The offseason acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Derek_Lee_(baseball)" title="Derek Lee (baseball)">Derek Lee</a> (who was acquired in a trade with Florida for <a href="/wiki/Hee-seop_Choi" title="Hee-seop Choi">Hee-seop Choi</a>) and the return of <a href="/wiki/Greg_Maddux" title="Greg Maddux">Greg Maddux</a> only bolstered these expectations. Despite a mid-season deal for <a href="/wiki/Nomar_Garciaparra" title="Nomar Garciaparra">Nomar Garciaparra</a>, misfortune struck the Cubs again. They led the Wild Card by 1.5 games over <a href="/wiki/2004_San_Francisco_Giants_season" title="2004 San Francisco Giants season">San Francisco</a> and <a href="/wiki/2004_Houston_Astros_season" title="2004 Houston Astros season">Houston</a> on September 25. On that day, both teams lost, giving the Cubs a chance at increasing the lead to 2.5 games with only eight games remaining in the season, but reliever <a href="/wiki/LaTroy_Hawkins" title="LaTroy Hawkins">LaTroy Hawkins</a> blew a save to the <a href="/wiki/2004_New_York_Mets_season" title="2004 New York Mets season">Mets</a>, and the Cubs lost the game in extra innings. The defeat seemingly deflated the team, as they proceeded to drop six of their last eight games as the Astros won the Wild Card.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:146px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RyanDempster.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/RyanDempster.jpg/144px-RyanDempster.jpg" width="144" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/RyanDempster.jpg/216px-RyanDempster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/RyanDempster.jpg/288px-RyanDempster.jpg 2x" data-file-width="804" data-file-height="1334" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RyanDempster.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dempster emerged in 2004 and became the Cubs\' regular closer.</div></div></div>\n<p>Despite the fact that the Cubs had won 89 games, this fallout was decidedly unlovable, as the Cubs traded superstar <a href="/wiki/Sammy_Sosa" title="Sammy Sosa">Sammy Sosa</a> after he had left the season\'s final game early and then lied about it publicly. Already a controversial figure in the clubhouse after his <a href="/wiki/Sammy_Sosa#Corked_bat_incident" title="Sammy Sosa">corked-bat incident</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Sammy\'s actions alienated much of his once strong fan base as well as the few teammates still on good terms with him, (many teammates grew tired of Sosa playing loud <a href="/wiki/Salsa_music" title="Salsa music">salsa music</a> in the locker room) and possibly tarnished his place in Cubs\' lore for years to come.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The disappointing season also saw fans start to become frustrated with the constant injuries to ace pitchers Mark Prior and Kerry Wood. Additionally, the 2004 season led to the departure of popular commentator <a href="/wiki/Steve_Stone_(baseball)" title="Steve Stone (baseball)">Steve Stone</a>, who had become increasingly critical of management during broadcasts and was verbally attacked by reliever <a href="/wiki/Kent_Mercker" title="Kent Mercker">Kent Mercker</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Things were no better in <a href="/wiki/2005_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="2005 Major League Baseball season">2005</a>, despite a career year from first baseman <a href="/wiki/Derrek_Lee" title="Derrek Lee">Derrek Lee</a> and the emergence of closer <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Dempster" title="Ryan Dempster">Ryan Dempster</a>. The club struggled and suffered more key injuries, only managing to win 79 games after being picked by many to be a serious contender for the N.L. pennant. In 2006, bottom fell out as the Cubs finished 66\xe2\x80\x9396, last in the <a href="/wiki/NL_Central" class="mw-redirect" title="NL Central">NL Central</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="2007.E2.80.932008:_Back_to_back_division_titles"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2007\xe2\x80\x932008:_Back_to_back_division_titles">2007\xe2\x80\x932008: Back to back division titles</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alfonso_Soriano_4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Alfonso_Soriano_4.jpg/175px-Alfonso_Soriano_4.jpg" width="175" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Alfonso_Soriano_4.jpg/263px-Alfonso_Soriano_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Alfonso_Soriano_4.jpg/350px-Alfonso_Soriano_4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="799" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alfonso_Soriano_4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alfonso Soriano signed with the club in 2007</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/2007_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2007 Chicago Cubs season">2007 Chicago Cubs season</a> and <a href="/wiki/2008_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2008 Chicago Cubs season">2008 Chicago Cubs season</a></div>\n<p>After finishing last in the NL Central with 66 wins in 2006, the Cubs re-tooled and went from "worst to first" in 2007. In the offseason they signed <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_Soriano" title="Alfonso Soriano">Alfonso Soriano</a> to a contract at eight years for $136 million,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and replaced manager <a href="/wiki/Dusty_Baker" title="Dusty Baker">Dusty Baker</a> with fiery veteran manager <a href="/wiki/Lou_Piniella" title="Lou Piniella">Lou Piniella</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> After a rough start, which included a brawl between <a href="/wiki/Michael_Barrett_(baseball)" title="Michael Barrett (baseball)">Michael Barrett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Zambrano" title="Carlos Zambrano">Carlos Zambrano</a>, the Cubs overcame the <a href="/wiki/2007_Milwaukee_Brewers_season" title="2007 Milwaukee Brewers season">Milwaukee Brewers</a>, who had led the division for most of the season. The Cubs traded Barrett to the Padres, and later acquired catcher <a href="/wiki/Jason_Kendall" title="Jason Kendall">Jason Kendall</a> from Oakland. Kendall was highly successful with his management of the pitching rotation and helped at the plate as well. By September, <a href="/wiki/Geovany_Soto" title="Geovany Soto">Geovany Soto</a> became the full-time starter behind the plate, replacing the veteran Kendall. Winning streaks in June and July, coupled with a pair of dramatic, late-inning wins against the <a href="/wiki/2007_Cincinnati_Reds_season" title="2007 Cincinnati Reds season">Reds</a>, led to the Cubs ultimately clinching the NL Central with a record of 85\xe2\x80\x9377. They met <a href="/wiki/2007_Arizona_Diamondbacks_season" title="2007 Arizona Diamondbacks season">Arizona</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2007_National_League_Division_Series" title="2007 National League Division Series">NLDS</a>, but controversy followed as Piniella, in a move that has since come under scrutiny,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> pulled Carlos Zambrano after the sixth inning of a pitcher\'s duel with <a href="/wiki/Arizona_Diamondbacks" title="Arizona Diamondbacks">D-Backs</a> <a href="/wiki/Ace_(baseball)" title="Ace (baseball)">ace</a> <a href="/wiki/Brandon_Webb" title="Brandon Webb">Brandon Webb</a>, to "....save Zambrano for (a potential) Game 4." The Cubs, however, were unable to come through, losing the first game and eventually stranding over 30 baserunners in a three-game Arizona sweep.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zambrano2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Zambrano2.JPG/180px-Zambrano2.JPG" width="180" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Zambrano2.JPG/270px-Zambrano2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Zambrano2.JPG/360px-Zambrano2.JPG 2x" data-file-width="467" data-file-height="319" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zambrano2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carlos Zambrano warming up before a game.</div></div></div><p>The Tribune company, in financial distress, was acquired by real-estate mogul Sam Zell in December 2007. This acquisition included the Cubs. However, Zell did not take an active part in running the baseball franchise, instead concentrating on putting together a deal to sell it.\n</p><p>The Cubs successfully defended their National League Central title in <a href="/wiki/2008_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="2008 Major League Baseball season">2008</a>, going to the postseason in consecutive years for the first time since 1906\xe2\x80\x9308. The offseason was dominated by three months of unsuccessful trade talks with the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles" title="Baltimore Orioles">Orioles</a> involving 2B <a href="/wiki/Brian_Roberts_(baseball)" title="Brian Roberts (baseball)">Brian Roberts</a>, as well as the signing of <a href="/wiki/Chunichi_Dragons" title="Chunichi Dragons">Chunichi Dragons</a> star <a href="/wiki/Kosuke_Fukudome" title="Kosuke Fukudome">Kosuke Fukudome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The team recorded their 10,000th win in April, while establishing an early division lead. <a href="/wiki/Reed_Johnson" title="Reed Johnson">Reed Johnson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jim_Edmonds" title="Jim Edmonds">Jim Edmonds</a> were added early on and <a href="/wiki/Rich_Harden" title="Rich Harden">Rich Harden</a> was acquired from the <a href="/wiki/2008_Oakland_Athletics_season" title="2008 Oakland Athletics season">Oakland Athletics</a> in early July.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The Cubs headed into the All-Star break with the N.L.\'s best record, and tied the league record with eight representatives to the <a href="/wiki/2008_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="2008 Major League Baseball All-Star Game">All-Star game</a>, including catcher Geovany Soto, who was named <a href="/wiki/Rookie_of_the_Year_(award)" title="Rookie of the Year (award)">Rookie of the Year</a>. The Cubs took control of the division by sweeping a four-game series in Milwaukee. On September 14, in a game moved to <a href="/wiki/Miller_Park_(Milwaukee)" class="mw-redirect" title="Miller Park (Milwaukee)">Miller Park</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Ike" title="Hurricane Ike">Hurricane Ike</a>, Zambrano pitched a no-hitter against the <a href="/wiki/2008_Houston_Astros_season" title="2008 Houston Astros season">Astros</a>, and six days later the team clinched by beating <a href="/wiki/2008_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="2008 St. Louis Cardinals season">St. Louis</a> at Wrigley. The club ended the season with a 97\xe2\x80\x9364 record<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and met <a href="/wiki/2008_Los_Angeles_Dodgers_season" title="2008 Los Angeles Dodgers season">Los Angeles</a> in the <a href="/wiki/2008_National_League_Division_Series" title="2008 National League Division Series">NLDS</a>. The heavily favored Cubs took an early lead in Game 1, but <a href="/wiki/James_Loney_(baseball)" class="mw-redirect" title="James Loney (baseball)">James Loney</a>\'s grand slam off <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Dempster" title="Ryan Dempster">Ryan Dempster</a> changed the series\' momentum. Chicago committed numerous critical errors and were outscored 20\xe2\x80\x936 in a Dodger sweep, which provided yet another sudden ending.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="The_Ricketts_era_.282009.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Ricketts_era_(2009\xe2\x80\x93present)">The Ricketts era (2009\xe2\x80\x93present)</span></h3>\n<p>The Ricketts family acquired a majority interest in the Cubs in 2009, ending the Tribune years. Apparently handcuffed by the Tribune\'s bankruptcy and the sale of the club to the Ricketts siblings, led by chairman <a href="/wiki/Thomas_S._Ricketts" title="Thomas S. Ricketts">Thomas S. Ricketts</a>, the <a href="/wiki/2009_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2009 Chicago Cubs season">Cubs</a>\' quest for a NL Central three-peat started with notice that there would be less invested into contracts than in previous years. Chicago engaged <a href="/wiki/2009_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="2009 St. Louis Cardinals season">St. Louis</a> in a see-saw battle for first place into August 2009, but the Cardinals played to a torrid 20\xe2\x80\x936 pace that month, designating their rivals to battle in the Wild Card race, from which they were eliminated in the season\'s final week. The Cubs were plagued by injuries in 2009, and were only able to field their Opening Day starting lineup three times the entire season. Third baseman Aramis Ram\xc3\xadrez injured his throwing shoulder in an early May game against the Milwaukee Brewers, sidelining him until early July and forcing journeyman players like <a href="/wiki/Mike_Fontenot" title="Mike Fontenot">Mike Fontenot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Miles" title="Aaron Miles">Aaron Miles</a> into more prominent roles. Additionally, key players like Derrek Lee (who still managed to hit .306 with 35 HR and 111 RBI that season), Alfonso Soriano, and Geovany Soto also nursed nagging injuries. The Cubs posted a winning record (83\xe2\x80\x9378) for the third consecutive season, the first time the club had done so since <a href="/wiki/1972_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="1972 Chicago Cubs season">1972</a>, and a new era of ownership under the Ricketts family was approved by MLB owners in early October.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="2010.E2.80.932014:_The_decline_and_rebuild"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2010\xe2\x80\x932014:_The_decline_and_rebuild">2010\xe2\x80\x932014: The decline and rebuild</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/2010_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2010 Chicago Cubs season">2010 Chicago Cubs season</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2011 Chicago Cubs season">2011 Chicago Cubs season</a>, <a href="/wiki/2012_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2012 Chicago Cubs season">2012 Chicago Cubs season</a>, <a href="/wiki/2013_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2013 Chicago Cubs season">2013 Chicago Cubs season</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2014_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2014 Chicago Cubs season">2014 Chicago Cubs season</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Starlin_Castro_09-05-2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Starlin_Castro_09-05-2010.jpg/170px-Starlin_Castro_09-05-2010.jpg" width="170" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Starlin_Castro_09-05-2010.jpg/255px-Starlin_Castro_09-05-2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Starlin_Castro_09-05-2010.jpg/340px-Starlin_Castro_09-05-2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="484" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Starlin_Castro_09-05-2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Starlin Castro during his 2010 rookie season.</div></div></div><p>Rookie <a href="/wiki/Starlin_Castro" title="Starlin Castro">Starlin Castro</a> debuted in early May (2010) as the starting shortstop. However, the club played poorly in the early season, finding themselves 10 games under .500 at the end of June. In addition, long-time ace Carlos Zambrano was pulled from a game against the White Sox on June 25 after a tirade and shoving match with Derrek Lee, and was suspended indefinitely by Jim Hendry, who called the conduct "unacceptable." On August 22, Lou Piniella, who had already announced his retirement at the end of the season, announced that he would leave the Cubs prematurely to take care of his sick mother. <a href="/wiki/Mike_Quade" title="Mike Quade">Mike Quade</a> took over as the interim manager for the final 37 games of the year. Despite being well out of playoff contention the Cubs went 24\xe2\x80\x9313 under Quade, the best record in baseball during that 37 game stretch, earning Quade the manager position going forward on October 19.\n</p><p>On December 3, 2010, Cubs broadcaster and former third baseman, <a href="/wiki/Ron_Santo" title="Ron Santo">Ron Santo</a>, died due to complications from bladder cancer and diabetes. He spent 13 seasons as a player with the Cubs, and at the time of his death was regarded as one of the greatest players not in the Hall of Fame.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> He was posthumously elected to the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame" class="mw-redirect" title="Major League Baseball Hall of Fame">Major League Baseball Hall of Fame</a> in 2012.\n</p><p>Despite trading for pitcher <a href="/wiki/Matt_Garza" title="Matt Garza">Matt Garza</a> and signing free-agent slugger <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Pe%C3%B1a" title="Carlos Pe\xc3\xb1a">Carlos Pe\xc3\xb1a</a>, the Cubs finished the <a href="/wiki/2011_Chicago_Cubs_season" title="2011 Chicago Cubs season">2011 season</a> 20 games under .500 with a record of 71\xe2\x80\x9391. Weeks after the season came to an end, the club was rejuvenated in the form of a new philosophy, as new owner Tom Ricketts signed <a href="/wiki/Theo_Epstein" title="Theo Epstein">Theo Epstein</a> away from the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> naming him club President and giving him a five-year contract worth over $18 million, and subsequently discharged manager Mike Quade.
